<div id="review_1875177233" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1875177233" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1875177233?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽ by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=made-me-think">made-me-think</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=literary-stuff">literary-stuff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1875177233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9399615276904186903">This enigmatic literary fiction novel does a great job of playing with perceptions. I pulled out this short Booker Prize novel one night, thinking I'd just read a bit to get a feel for it, to know what to tell my book club about it, since I needed to suggest a choice of 4 or 5 books to my book club the next day for their vote. A few hours later I finished the book, moved but a little bewildered. <br/><br/>In the first fifty pages the narrator, Tony, tells of some events in his high school and college days</span>
  <span id="freeText9399615276904186903" style="display:none">This enigmatic literary fiction novel does a great job of playing with perceptions. I pulled out this short Booker Prize novel one night, thinking I'd just read a bit to get a feel for it, to know what to tell my book club about it, since I needed to suggest a choice of 4 or 5 books to my book club the next day for their vote. A few hours later I finished the book, moved but a little bewildered. <br /><br />In the first fifty pages the narrator, Tony, tells of some events in his high school and college days: a group of rather pretentious friends (who, for the most part, play at being intellectuals), a relationship with a girl, Veronica, that didn't work out, a friend's suicide, marriage, divorce, retirement ... it sounds pretty pedestrian when he tells about it, though Julian Barnes has a wonderful way with words.<br /><br />Then the next hundred pages happen: a bequest in a will. A short, enigmatic note from the woman who died. A renewed acquaintance with Veronica. More surprises. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And everything that Tony--and I as the reader--thought got upended. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><blockquote>How often do we tell our own life story? How often do we adjust, embellish, make sly cuts? And the longer life goes on, the fewer are those around to challenge our account, to remind us that our life is not our life, merely the story we have told about our life. Told to others, but—mainly—to ourselves.</blockquote>Some readers dislike Tony enough that it dampens or ruins their enjoyment of the book, but I had a great deal of sympathy for him.<blockquote>I had wanted life not to bother me too much, and had succeeded -- and how pitiful that was.<br /><br />But time...how time first grounds us and then confounds us. We thought we were being mature when we were only being safe. We imagined we were being responsible but we were only being cowardly. </blockquote>I've felt those feelings.<br /><br /><a href="http://andrewblackman.net/2012/05/the-sense-of-an-ending-explained/" rel="nofollow noopener">This blog</a> has one writer's explanation of what was really happening, and an <b>extremely</b> long but intensely interesting comment section where various readers chip in with their theories and insights, which gave me a lot of food for thought. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[How unreliable of a narrator is Tony? Can we even trust what he says in the end? I tend to think so, at the very end, but I think it's clear that, at the least, he's still oblivious about some things, like how Veronica felt about him when they were dating. The comments that gave me the most food for thought were the ones that suggested he, too, had a fling with Veronica's mother, and was the actual father of her child. I don't think I buy it, ultimately, but ...  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />So much food for thought ... and debate ... in this one!<br /><br />Content notes: Scattered F-bombs and some sexual content. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9399615276904186903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_272191167" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272191167" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272191167?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272191167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4845088410293611474">This book got under my skin. Not in the negative way, like what Tony, the narrator, may be doing, or trying to do, to Veronica, who 40 years ago was his first serious girlfriend, but in the way he describes how his ex-wife would dress a chicken -- slipping butter and herbs under the skin, with a delicate hand, never breaking the outer layer. I was hooked from the first page and even when I wasn't reading it, I was thinking about it, even in my sleep, or, more likely, semi-sleep. I was pulled int</span>
  <span id="freeText4845088410293611474" style="display:none">This book got under my skin. Not in the negative way, like what Tony, the narrator, may be doing, or trying to do, to Veronica, who 40 years ago was his first serious girlfriend, but in the way he describes how his ex-wife would dress a chicken -- slipping butter and herbs under the skin, with a delicate hand, never breaking the outer layer. I was hooked from the first page and even when I wasn't reading it, I was thinking about it, even in my sleep, or, more likely, semi-sleep. I was pulled into the dream of someone else's life, like the best novels do to the reader, and I stayed there.<br /><br />Though the book at first reminded me greatly of Ford Madox Ford's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7628.The_Good_Soldier" title="The Good Soldier by Ford Madox Ford" rel="nofollow noopener">The Good Soldier</a> (my guess is that was intentional), somewhere after the middle, it stopped feeling like it altogether and became its own entity. At this point it also started to drag a tiny bit (so maybe my rating is really <b>4 and 3/4 stars</b>), but that time was brief. <br /><br />While I didn't completely see the ending that arrived, I did have a clue, as I was reading very carefully, with a lot of attention, as if it were "The Good Soldier." (That's not to say that plot is essential to this story, because it's not at all, except in the ways the narrator has to adjust his thinking.) Still, there were surprises, as there is in all great fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4845088410293611474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_550263824" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/550263824" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/550263824?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/38313161-the-sense-of-an-ending">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Kris, Jim, Ian Graye</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nataliya by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/17961194-iffletoe">Iffletoe</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=booker-winners-and-nominees">booker-winners-and-nominees</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2013-reads">2013-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer550263824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5719239487306148963"><br/>Julian Barnes' <i>The Sense of an Ending</i> has a lot packed in the short 150 or so pages. <br/><br/>Memory and history, responsibility and blame, deceit, misunderstandings, aging, guilt, remorse - and, of course, a safely passive coasting on the smooth sailing surface of life, occasionally interrupted by the tidal waves of unexpected upheavals and disturbances, just like <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Severn_bore' rel='nofollow noopener'>Severn Bore</a>, seen once by Tony Webster and Veronica.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380411776i/809353.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><blockquote>
  <i>"We live with such easy assumptions, don’t we? For instance, that memory equals events</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText5719239487306148963" style="display:none"><br>Julian Barnes' <i>The Sense of an Ending</i> has a lot packed in the short 150 or so pages. <br><br>Memory and history, responsibility and blame, deceit, misunderstandings, aging, guilt, remorse - and, of course, a safely passive coasting on the smooth sailing surface of life, occasionally interrupted by the tidal waves of unexpected upheavals and disturbances, just like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Severn_bore" rel="nofollow noopener">Severn Bore</a>, seen once by Tony Webster and Veronica.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380411776i/809353.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><blockquote>
  <i>"We live with such easy assumptions, don’t we? For instance, that memory equals events plus time. But it’s all much odder than this. Who was it said that memory is what we thought we’d forgotten? And it ought to be obvious to us that time doesn’t act as a fixative, rather as a solvent. But it’s not convenient — it’s not useful — to believe this; it doesn’t help us get on with our lives; so we ignore it."</i>
</blockquote>It is the story of <b>fickleness and subjectivity of memory that creates the unreliable histories we tell ourselves</b>; the dissonance between what happened and what stories we choose to tell ourselves - because such unconscious lies are often what we just happen to need to feel alright about ourselves. <br><br><b>And so we construct our own memories and write our life stories the way it suits us.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"How often do we tell our own life story? How often do we adjust, embellish, make sly cuts? And the longer life goes on, the fewer are those around to challenge our account, to remind us that our life is not our life, merely the story we have told about our life. Told to others, but — mainly — to ourselves."</i>
</blockquote>It's the story that touches on <b>allotting responsibility and blame - be it for the Great War or a breakup or a divorce or a suicide</b> - and learn that the answers may not always be there. We can come up with a catchy and cheeky answers <i>('History is the lies of the winners', pretentiously and predictably states teenage Tony Webster with all the world-weariness of a sixteen-year-old)</i> - but ultimately, the answers are never clear-cut, and everything is in the grey zone, and the realization is that of a sixty-year-old Tony:<blockquote>
  <i>"History isn’t the lies of the victors, as I once glibly assured Old Joe Hunt; I know that now. It’s more the memories of the survivors, most of whom are neither victorious nor defeated."</i>
</blockquote>It's a melancholic reflection of the reality of aging and the little differences between 'settling for it' and 'accepting reality'. And, what's pathetically and sadly true, we fail to really grow and change at the end of our life stories - after all, <b>Life is not really Literature</b>. Yeah, it's not a book to read when you're feeling a bit down on yourself. <b>Because at the end <i>"what you end up remembering isn’t always the same as what you have witnessed."</i></b><blockquote>
  <i>"That’s one of the central problems of history, isn’t it, sir? The question of subjective versus objective interpretation, the fact that we need to know the history of the historian in order to understand the version that is being put in front of us."</i>
</blockquote>With this introduction, we take a dive into the mind of Tony Webster - a self-centered average guy who, like the rest of us, uses the subjectivity of memory to be the hero in his story, to be who he needs himself to be, to unconsciously tinker with the events until they seem just right. 'Yes, I remember exactly what happened!' is not that reliable, and Tony comes to learn that. Whether he actually takes something important from this experience - well, that's debatable<blockquote>
  <i>"It strikes me that this may be one of the differences between youth and age: when we are young, we invent different futures for ourselves; when we are old, we invent different pasts for others."</i>
</blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380411776i/809354._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>We meet Tony at sixteen, through the recollection of his almost five decades older self. The older Tony constantly interrupts his own narration to remind us of the subjectivity of his memory, setting himself up as the ultimate unreliable narrator. This story resembles a coming-of-age book at the beginning <i>(with all those pseudo- and not-so-pseudo-intellectual teenagers in the British prep school in the 1960s waxing on and on about philosophical matters with the smugness inherent to the adolescence)</i> - but it turns out to be anything but.<blockquote>
  <i>"Discovering, for example, that as the witnesses to your life diminish, there is less corroboration, and therefore less certainty, as to what you are or have been. Even if you have assiduously kept records— in words, sound, pictures— you may find that you have attended to the wrong kind of record-keeping."</i>
</blockquote><b>Tony Webster is pathetic, self-centered and self-righteous.</b> His life did not turn out the way he thought it'd be (<i>"This was another of our fears: that Life wouldn’t turn out to be like Literature."</i>)- and he has been coasting through it, just an average guy leading an average life, far from the inspirations he may have had when back in his teens he was friends with a bright young philosopher <i>(yes, a bit full of himself - but who isn't at that age?)</i> <b>Adrian Finn</b>, who produces such pompous little gems as this one: <blockquote>
  <i>"History is that certainty produced at the point where the imperfections of memory meet the inadequacies of documentation."</i>
</blockquote>And then, a few years later, Tony's university girlfriend <b>Veronica Ford</b><i>(the one he views through the prism of his memories as a supreme bitch)</i> dumped him and dated Adrian, and then Adrian committed suicide, and then there were four unexciting mediocre decades, and now the above ex-girlfriend's mother left Adrian's diary to Tony - but the ex-girlfriend is not willing to part with what Tony comes to view as his rightful legacy. And along the path to reclaim that diary Tony embarks on a quest to turn remorse into guilt and guilt into forgiveness - in the most self-centered way possible. Along the way he also toys with shouldering responsibility for what happened in the lives of Veronica and Adrian - but, as I see it, this over-estimation of his own importance is yet another one of his memory delusions and instead he may be on the sidelines of this story, regardless of what his unreliable memory tells him his life story should be.<blockquote>
  <i>"Perhaps I just feel safer with the history that’s been more or less agreed upon. Or perhaps it’s that same paradox again: the history that happens underneath our noses ought to be the clearest, and yet it’s the most deliquescent. We live in time, it bounds us and defines us, and time is supposed to measure history, isn’t it? But if we can’t understand time, can’t grasp its mysteries of pace and progress, what chance do we have with history— even our own small, personal, largely undocumented piece of it?"</i>
</blockquote>But maybe, just maybe, the aging Tony Webster will learn something from the trip to the past he takes on his quest to recover Adrian's journal. But ultimately it's not about Tony at all; Tony is just a slate on which to project the final thoughts, the final lines of this novel that harbor a bit of hope for the majority of us, floaters on the safe waters of life, who may or may not meet their Severn Bore.<blockquote>
  <i>"There is accumulation. There is responsibility. And beyond these, there is unrest. There is great unrest."</i>
</blockquote>This story is written with enough irony and melancholy to sustain a book of a much larger size. It's insanely quotable, to the point where you begin to suspect that certain lines were thrown in by the author with expectations of their future quotability factor. The language is smart and exquisite, sometimes a bit sardonic, sometimes a bit pedantic, and sometimes painfully genuine. <b>Love this book or hate it - but you cannot deny that the writing is quite excellent.</b><br><br>.......<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380411776i/809355.jpg" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg">....<br>And to close off, I want to go back to the image of the Severn Bore, the natural phenomenon that unsettled Tony back in his youth and still may be the disturbance that we all need from time to time. <b>Great unrest, so to speak.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"I don't think I can properly convey the effect that moment had on me. It wasn't like a tornado or an earthquake (not that I'd witnessed either) – nature being violent and destructive, putting us in our place. It was more unsettling because it looked and felt quietly wrong, as if some small lever of the universe had been pressed, and here, just for these minutes, nature was reversed, and time with it."</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="5719239487306148963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1841978038" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1841978038" />
    <a title="Lizzy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30575403-lizzy"><img alt="Lizzy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512052362p2/30575403.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1841978038?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lizzy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lizzy" href="/user/show/30575403-lizzy">Lizzy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307947727" href="/book/show/13536315-the-sense-of-an-ending">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=read-2017">read-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=classics-literay-fiction">classics-literay-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=stars-5">stars-5</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1841978038" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11937206063485936866"><i>
  <blockquote>Again, I must stress that this is my reading now of what happened then. Or rather, my memory now of my reading then of what was happening at the time.</blockquote>
</i>From the first page, I was carried away by Julian Barnes’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending' title='The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sense of an Ending</a> and its wonderful story. For such a short novel it seems to waste no words, and only speeds between breaths to tell us about the capriciousness of our memories. I think it cannot be truer that memory is a fickle friend. More than a story, this was a lyrical lesson</span>
  <span id="freeText11937206063485936866" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>Again, I must stress that this is my reading now of what happened then. Or rather, my memory now of my reading then of what was happening at the time.</blockquote>
</i>From the first page, I was carried away by Julian Barnes’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending" title="The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sense of an Ending</a> and its wonderful story. For such a short novel it seems to waste no words, and only speeds between breaths to tell us about the capriciousness of our memories. I think it cannot be truer that memory is a fickle friend. More than a story, this was a lyrical lesson with a very precise rhythm of life and time. What time? The forgotten, that that has long passed and that we not always know how to remember. Yes, memory often enough plays tricks on us, but we go on living.<br /><i>
  <blockquote>We live in time -- it holds us and moulds us -- but I've never felt I understood it very well. And I’m not referring to theories about how it bends and doubles back, or may exist elsewhere in parallel versions. No, I mean ordinary, everyday time, which clocks and watches assure us passes regularly: tick-tock, click-clock. Is there anything more plausible than a second hand? And yet it takes only the smallest pleasure or pain to teach us time's malleability. Some emotions speed it up, others slow it down occasionally, it seems to go missing – until the eventual point when it really does go missing, never to return.</blockquote>
</i><br />If you are old enough to wonder why you forgot those long ago times, you will enjoy this book. No, I do not mean old in years, but in memories. <i>‘It strikes me that this may be one of the differences between youth and age: when we are young, we invent different futures for ourselves; when we are old, we invent different pasts for others.’</i> Did you ever wonder about your first remembrances or the memory of your first love? I do it all the time, and how we try or not to remember and how we choose to recollect those long past times. So we live in a make believe scenery where we create unreliable histories that when we less expect come back to surprise us. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>"How often do we tell our own life story? How often do we adjust, embellish, make sly cuts? And the longer life goes on, the fewer are those around to challenge our account, to remind us that our life is not our life, merely the story we have told about our life. Told to others, but - mainly - to ourselves."</blockquote>
</i><br />While I ran through the pages of <i>The Sense of an Ending</i>, I could recognize myself often in Tony’s feelings and wonders. Not that our lives have that much in common, besides a failed marriage. I could sympathize with him when he relived his life through older eyes that imagined saw more but often forgot or mislead him. <i>‘Perhaps I just feel safer with the history that's been more or less agreed upon. Or perhaps it's that same paradox again: the history that happens underneath our noses ought to be the clearest, and yet it's the most deliquescent.’</i> But his losses I felt as my own, I think when I read his recollections I imagined my own instead. Or they were simply mixed up in Barnes’s words.<br /><i>
  <blockquote>Your winnings accumulate. But do your losses? Not at the racetrack - there, you just lose your original stake. But in life? Perhaps here different rules apply. You bet a relationship, it fails; you go on to the next relationship, it fails too: and maybe what you lose is not two simple minus sums... Life isn't just addition and subtraction. There's also accumulation, the multiplication, of loss, of failure.</blockquote>
</i><br />Thus, do we really build our own memories and write our life stories the way it better suits us? I wonder. Perhaps, or better yet, probably, we do as a protection from useless sufferings. But do you ever ponder what we would imagine if we were in fact in control of our past? Certainly not disillusionment or a lack of sense regarding what we aimed to accomplish. We have to believe our lives were worth living, so we may yes lie to ourselves. What else can a human being do?<br /><i>
  <blockquote>Who has it said that memory is what we thought we'd forgotten? And it ought to be obvious to us that time doesn't act as a fixative, rather as a solvent. But it's not convenient - it's not useful - to believe this; it doesn't help us get on with our lives; so we ignore it.</blockquote>
</i><br />Tony Webster’s memory might come through as a melancholic reflection on the reality of aging, but can aging be felt differently? I wonder as I feel myself getting older every day and as I feel the elapse of time more accurately. Is Barnes being too melodramatic? It depends on your memories and how you remember then, I answer myself. And I know that there are times when it’s best not to go back into the past, not try to recollect what went wrong or the missteps we took, but to reinvent what how we existed and live what life has still to offer us so as not to live in eternal gloom.<br /><i>
  <blockquote>I had been tempted, somehow, by the notion that we could excise most of our separate existences, could cut and splice the magnetic tape on which our lives are recorded, go back to that fork in the path and take the road less travelled, or rather not travelled at all. Instead, I had just left common sense behind. Old fool, I said to myself. And there's not fool like an old fool:...</blockquote>
</i><br />And, is it not pathetic and sad when we fail to grow up and change at the end of our life stories, after all, <i>‘life is not really literature’</i>. But if you pay attention, you can feel time pass by and make the best of it.<br /><i>
  <blockquote>I know this much: that there is objective time, but also subjective time, the kind you wear on the inside of your wrist, next to where the pulse lies. And this personal time, which is the true time, is measured in your relationship to memory. So when this strange thing happened - when these new memories suddenly came upon me - it was as if, for that moment, time had been placed in reverse. As if, for that moment, the river run upstream.</blockquote>
</i><br />What I realized as I closed the last page is that <i>The Sense of an Ending</i> is a book to be read in a sunny afternoon and not when you're feeling down. I am glad I have been feeling cheerful lately, because at the end what you end up reminiscing is not always what you wanted to remember or what you taught you witnessed. <br /><br />Highly recommended!<br />____</span>
  <a data-text-id="11937206063485936866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_237474497" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/237474497" />
    <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em"><img alt="Jennifer (aka EM)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510513814p2/1393336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237474497?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer (aka EM)" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em">Jennifer (aka EM)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237474497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9771270655073986239">Maybe, like Tony, I just don't get it, but this was a whole lot of Man Booker-winning to-do about very little. <br/><br/>Pretentious, upper middle-class schoolboys behave badly, and -- through too much ego and too little self-knowledge and empathy, too many book smarts and not enough life experience -- inflict cruelty on ex-girlfriends and others as they cavalierly grow out of their coddled adolescence into a ho-hum average life. It then comes back to haunt them - or one of them, anyway - in late middle-a</span>
  <span id="freeText9771270655073986239" style="display:none">Maybe, like Tony, I just don't get it, but this was a whole lot of Man Booker-winning to-do about very little. <br /><br />Pretentious, upper middle-class schoolboys behave badly, and -- through too much ego and too little self-knowledge and empathy, too many book smarts and not enough life experience -- inflict cruelty on ex-girlfriends and others as they cavalierly grow out of their coddled adolescence into a ho-hum average life. It then comes back to haunt them - or one of them, anyway - in late middle-age, and as memories are jogged and regrets bubble to the surface, insight is finally gained. Sort of. It's really a dull spark of understanding nested in about the same amount of egocentric self-pity that it started out disguised by.<br /><br />I guess it's a clever character study, but I didn't and don't like Tony. I think that *is* the point. I really just wanted to give him a smack upside the head and tell him to stop whining and get over himself, kind of like his ex-wife did. <br /><br />All life has tragedy - and some lives here have *true* tragedy, which Tony fails to recognize even as he seem to be recognizing it (so I guess that's pretty clever of Barnes, but sheesh ...). <br /><br />Tony's real insight seems to be the recognition that he's failed to live up to his own pompous and overblown expectations of greatness for himself. He's led an average life. And along the way, through his own obtuseness and arrogance, he's inflicted a not-inconsiderable degree of harm, which seems to be of less consequence to him in the grand scheme of things than the fact that he's lived a life of complacent mediocrity.<br /><br />But then, Barnes leaves us there, with this fellow sighing into his beer and chips. I feel myself asking - but what's next? What this guy does NEXT will tell the real story of his character. I feel ... short-changed; unfulfilled. Harumph.<br /><br />Subject: The last chapter<br /><br />Dear Tony: The cure for your loneliness and sense of ennui is to pull your head out of your ass and go out and do something of value with the time you have left. Turn that insight into action that will do someone some good (and I don't mean just leaving a sizeable tip at the pub, either).<br /><br />Love, Jen<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9771270655073986239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2263210902" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2263210902" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63168028-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516912354p2/63168028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2263210902?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/63168028-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2263210902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17398711098866214766"><b>Disposition</b><br/>Julian Barnes’ writing in this short novel speaks loudly in an often quiet reflective allegory of memory and loss. Overtly philosophical, Tony Webster, tells of his youth as a schoolboy, with two old friends Alex and Colin, and the new kid, Adrian Finn. How their ideological sense of the world met with affectations as they sought to impress each other and teachers on various subjects, although always acceding to Adrian’s intelligent and apparent self-awareness. Nearing the end of scho</span>
  <span id="freeText17398711098866214766" style="display:none"><b>Disposition</b><br />Julian Barnes’ writing in this short novel speaks loudly in an often quiet reflective allegory of memory and loss. Overtly philosophical, Tony Webster, tells of his youth as a schoolboy, with two old friends Alex and Colin, and the new kid, Adrian Finn. How their ideological sense of the world met with affectations as they sought to impress each other and teachers on various subjects, although always acceding to Adrian’s intelligent and apparent self-awareness. Nearing the end of school there was a sense of being <i>“kept in a holding pen waiting to be released”</i>, which suggests an expectation of passion, action and success in the future. Quite unfulfilled then, that as an older man he reflected that <blockquote>
  <i> “Sometimes I think the purpose of life is to reconcile us to its eventual loss by wearing us down, by proving, however long it takes, that life isn’t all it’s cracked up to be.” </i>
</blockquote> A sad reflection that life isn’t a simple calculation of additions and subtractions but now and again a multiplication of loss. Tony Webster is a complex character, seen through one lens as he progresses through life and a different one looking back over time after he receives a bequeathed gift from an old girlfriend’s mother. <br /><br />Tony has drifted through his career, marriage to Margaret, fatherhood, amicable divorce, and tedious retirement, until Veronica’s mother sent him £500 in a will and the promise of Adrian's diary. Veronica leaving Tony for Adrian left pain and deep resentment, causing him to write a letter to them both. A letter that now returned forty years later, does not align with his memory of its contents and which reads cruel and spiteful. In <i>The Sense of an Ending,</i> memory is the precarious matter to be traded between fact and fiction, how time changes memory and how true time is measured in your relationship to memory. When new memories came flooding back <i> “it was as if for that moment, time had been placed in reverse. As if, for that moment, the river ran upstream.”</i> Maybe our personalised memories of events and moments are all that really matter.<br /><br />Barnes creates a wonderful balance between empathy and frustration in watching a character wrestle with their decisions and behaviour when discarded as a love interest. Searching for answers when the smart thing to do is avoid contact and walk away. Failure to do so only has one outcome, more frustration, disappointment and unrest - exposing the neediness and truths that were better ignored. In broken personal relationships, do answers ever really help?<br /><br />The self-pitying doleful nature of Tony Webster is not a character easily liked and while his outlook on life is bleak there is a hope that he can find acceptance or resolution in the answers he seeks. There is a hope that he can enjoy what remains of his life rather than the nagging ineptitude of his life’s accumulation. For a new beginning, there needs to be a sense of an ending.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book and as a second read, I appreciated more the mastery of prose Julian Barnes displays. A deserved winner of a literature prize – The Man Booker prize in 2011. I would rate the book 4.5 stars and I would highly recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17398711098866214766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2969299145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2969299145" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2969299145?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2969299145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3527502194841261852">It seems inevitable that virtually every writer will at some point write a meditation on ageing and the role of engineered memory in defining identity. This is Julien Barnes contribution to a somewhat oversubscribed theme, one which is very popular with Booker judges, perhaps because they're generally of an an age when this theme is especially pertinent to them. <br/><br/>I'm not a great fan of minimalist prose so Barnes is never going to become one of my favourite novelists. I like my writers to be more </span>
  <span id="freeText3527502194841261852" style="display:none">It seems inevitable that virtually every writer will at some point write a meditation on ageing and the role of engineered memory in defining identity. This is Julien Barnes contribution to a somewhat oversubscribed theme, one which is very popular with Booker judges, perhaps because they're generally of an an age when this theme is especially pertinent to them. <br /><br />I'm not a great fan of minimalist prose so Barnes is never going to become one of my favourite novelists. I like my writers to be more painterly, more linguistically adventurous, more connected to the sensual world. Barnes doesn't have the gift for immersing you in the moment; his gift is more for providing the philosophical overview. (The best writers can do both.) It's essentially his wisdom that has to carry this novel because his characters aren't particularly credible except for cyphers for his ideas. (Would anyone with intelligence really have written the absurdly childish letter Alex writes? Would a grown woman really choose for no reason to be so obtuse and coy as Veronica in the final stretch of the novel? <br /><br /><br />There are lots of good quotes in this novel. In fact, I couldn't help feeling the quotes deserved a better novel. For this novel to work the reader is called upon to accept unlikely melodrama at all its pivotal moments. And there wasn't enough humour, as there would be in an early Amis novel, to help me surf through these overly contrived plot fountains. I had bought into the mystery element and was reading with some degree of intrigue but what a damp squid of a denouement! <br /><br />Some admiration. Little love. Ultimately another overrated Booker winner (though having just looked at the long list that year it didn't have much competition.) About 3.7 for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3527502194841261852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1841933488" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1841933488" />
    <a title="Liz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4125660-liz"><img alt="Liz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316269997p2/4125660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1841933488?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz" href="/user/show/4125660-liz">Liz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11881904-the-sense-of-an-ending">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4125660-liz?shelf=book-clubs">book-clubs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1841933488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11932776140170840200">This book literally grabbed me from the first page. I found myself highlighting phrase after phrase. The narrator starts the book looking back at his school days. He's got the presence of mind to understand exactly what he and the other lads were like in those days. He also has the understanding of how memory truly works. “...what you end up remembering isn't always the same as what you have witnessed.” Or this “ I need to return briefly to a few incidents that have grown into anecdotes.” <br/><br/>The wr</span>
  <span id="freeText11932776140170840200" style="display:none">This book literally grabbed me from the first page. I found myself highlighting phrase after phrase. The narrator starts the book looking back at his school days. He's got the presence of mind to understand exactly what he and the other lads were like in those days. He also has the understanding of how memory truly works. “...what you end up remembering isn't always the same as what you have witnessed.” Or this “ I need to return briefly to a few incidents that have grown into anecdotes.” <br /><br />The writing here is delicious. I want to offer up example after example just to show you. He continually returns to the concept of time and memory and his thoughts are spot on. It's a short book, but there's a lot packed in the pages. I'm wondering how much of my enjoyment is because Barnes is thinking about the same things I find myself pondering more frequently as I age. How much of what I remember is accurate? How can people at the same event have such different memories of it? <br /><br />Tony is dense and a bit of a lughead, thinking he can go back and change history. But Veronica was an enigma to both Tony and me. She expects him to understand without being told. He wasn't the only one who was clueless until the end. <br /><br />A deep book and one I highly recommend. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11932776140170840200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1085997526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1085997526" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1085997526?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1085997526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9894829382959080804">The Sense of an Ending is the type of British novel ALL OTHER AMERICAN NOVELS TREMBLE IN THE PRESENCE OF. It is blessed with an aura of flawless, impeccable English perfection; the prose is exquisitely clean & concise, GODLY by most-- especially my own-- standards. <br/><br/>It is an uncommon, unpolluted work that should be embedded in psychology books everywhere: the gears of life are described in their rare light, in degrees that, you must agree, can only possibly come from another world, or another dim</span>
  <span id="freeText9894829382959080804" style="display:none">The Sense of an Ending is the type of British novel ALL OTHER AMERICAN NOVELS TREMBLE IN THE PRESENCE OF. It is blessed with an aura of flawless, impeccable English perfection; the prose is exquisitely clean &amp; concise, GODLY by most-- especially my own-- standards. <br /><br />It is an uncommon, unpolluted work that should be embedded in psychology books everywhere: the gears of life are described in their rare light, in degrees that, you must agree, can only possibly come from another world, or another dimension. Barnes, like my other major European discovery of the year Michel Houellebecq, is a purveyor of human behavior, adding and utilizing British conventions of Modern English Literature in his own book. The occasion which hooks the reader, like in another perennial favorite by the sublime Kazuo Ishiguro's "Never Let Me Go" in which a little girl dances, a school teacher has a breakdown, is present in a similar, indeed also masterful scene, when a teacher actually tells his student he possesses the knowledge to teach the entire class. The reader won't let this one go from page 6. The knowledge, the expounding of it in our naïve brains, is terrifyingly constant: it radiates from the book like an individual's life force.<br /><br />The small details present in literature-- from Desdemona's handkerchief, to Aurora's spindle's needle, to, in this instance, a hateful childhood curse, a simple adolescent note (think, obviously "Atonement")-- those small and almost invisible details always seem to carry an immense heft, a terrible burden of the psychological kind. This conceit, this must, is also found in our gorgeous mini novel.<br /><br />"Sense" is at the apex of literary brilliance, my astute friends. &amp; the gushing, for me, shall simply, without a doubt, not end here...</span>
  <a data-text-id="9894829382959080804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16409124374558002901">Has it ever occurred to you that while you are complacently sitting, basking in the self acquired glory of wisdom, you chance upon something, like an incident, a person or a written word, which forces you to revisit your understanding and knowledge of the life as you know it? And then you gasp with a sudden disbelief at the ignorance which might have silently crept in and stayed along while you felt contented with your version of perceptions?   <span id="freeText16409124374558002901" style="display:none">Has it ever occurred to you that while you are complacently sitting, basking in the self acquired glory of wisdom, you chance upon something, like an incident, a person or a written word, which forces you to revisit your understanding and knowledge of the life as you know it? And then you gasp with a sudden disbelief at the ignorance which might have silently crept in and stayed along while you felt contented with your version of perceptions? I felt the same while reading this book. To say that I liked what I read would be an understatement. What I experienced was a sense of delight, in not just understanding through the memory of the narrator, but in understanding that as human beings, we, sometimes fabricate part of our memory to make up for a more suitable version of things for ourselves. And that while we do understand this, it is possible to be more cautious while recalling such memories and making a judgment.<br /></span>

<div id="review_2219520863" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2219520863" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2219520863?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=man-booker">man-booker</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2219520863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1761290860626710444">I find it appropriate that my last review of 2017 is titled The Sense of an Ending. Recently I read In the Noise of Time by Julian Barnes. Upon returning it to the library, two of my branch librarians asked me if I had ever read any of Barnes other works, and I answered, unfortunately, no. While reading about Shostakovich, I immediately fell in love with Barnes' flawless prose. My librarians know their patrons and reserved me The Sense of an Ending. A Man Booker winner, Barnes has wowed his read</span>
  <span id="freeText1761290860626710444" style="display:none">I find it appropriate that my last review of 2017 is titled The Sense of an Ending. Recently I read In the Noise of Time by Julian Barnes. Upon returning it to the library, two of my branch librarians asked me if I had ever read any of Barnes other works, and I answered, unfortunately, no. While reading about Shostakovich, I immediately fell in love with Barnes' flawless prose. My librarians know their patrons and reserved me The Sense of an Ending. A Man Booker winner, Barnes has wowed his readers with The Sense of an Ending, a book that ties up loose ends of an contemplative, older man's life, and, quite possibly, the ends of my reading year as well.<br /><br />Tony Webster is amicably divorced, still meeting his ex-wife Margaret for lunch once a week or so. He has a loving relationship with his daughter Susie and her two children, is retired from his job, and finds ample activities to keep him busy with his life. He refers to himself as an average adult with average interests, which Barnes depicts so eloquently. Recently, he has reminisced about his time in both preparatory school and university, which is depicted in part one of this book. In preparatory school, Tony forged close friendships with his gang in Alex, Colin, and newcomer Adrian, Adrian being the product of divorce and a brilliant mind who all of his classmates new had a bright future ahead. As expected, Adrian received a scholarship to Cambridge, Tony to Bristol, Colin to military service, and Alex working for his father. The four attempted to stay close throughout their college years, but in the 1960s before the internet, Tony admits that it was a strained effort to communicate with even his closest friends. Perhaps, if email had been around during Tony's university days, he and has friends would have been alerted to the emotional problems boiling under the surface of their lives.<br /><br />Upon entering Bristol, Tony is still unschooled in the ways of women. He is admittedly shy and has almost no experience in this arena. On a chance meeting looking for the Severn Boar, he meets Veronica Mary Elizabeth Ford, who is from a middle class family, snobby, and has more expertise in relationships than does Tony. The two have almost nothing in common down to the music they listen to and their academic interests. During a school vacation, Tony visits Veronica's family, and they proceed to humiliate him, everyone except the mother who apologizes for her family's rude behavior toward him. Unbeknownst to the dense Tony is that Veronica became smitten with the more academically refined Adrian on a visit to London. Following this visit home, the couple officially breaks up, Adrian writes to Tony for permission to see Veronica, and the ex-couple does not hear or see of each other for the next forty years.<br /><br />As Barnes quickly forwards his readers through time, he fills in the gaps in Tony's life. While still in college, Adrian unexpectedly commits suicide. The three remaining friends have no idea why a brilliant mind would tragically take his life at such a young age, and remain in the dark as to his motive. Forty years later, Margaret is still Tony's rock despite their breakup and the person he first calls upon when he receives an inheritance following the passing of Veronica's mother. The episode and letter accompanying the behest are puzzling at best, and Barnes through his expert prose does his best to fill in the gaps of the protagonists' lives without giving away any crucial details. He is an author who makes his readers think both about what happened and how the consequences of actions could spiral to create results even forty years in the future. The ending sections brought to mind another British master, Agatha Christie, who leaves her readers contemplating whodunit until the book's closing paragraphs. This is the mastery that Barnes has painted here, one that attempts to give his readers a sense of an ending and merited a Man Booker Award.<br /><br />Julian Barnes is a living masterful author whose work is constantly being nominated for international awards. I thank my goodreads friends for including his work in their year end reviews, piquing my own interest to read his work. I also give credit to my branch librarians who know my reading interests and reserved this book for me so that I could read it before the end of the year. Julian Barnes creates The Sense of an Ending indeed, both for Tony Webster and for my reading year. I will most definitely be reading more of his special work in the years to come.<br /><br />4+ stars<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1761290860626710444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_263311943" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263311943" />
    <a title="Fionnuala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala"><img alt="Fionnuala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368182051p2/5498525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/263311943?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fionnuala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fionnuala" href="/user/show/5498525-fionnuala">Fionnuala</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5498525-fionnuala?shelf=julianbarnes">julianbarnes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer263311943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15928468772678535883">Reviewed in January, 2012<br/><br/>One of the things I admire about Barnes is the pared down nature of his writing. Every word counts. <br/>The division of this novella into two parts also counts. The reader could start with Part Two and the book wouldn't be any less clear. In fact, possible answers to most of the questions raised at the end of the book can be found on rereading Part One. More enlightenment comes while rereading Part Two. <br/><br/>(The following paragraph may contain spoilers) <br/>As to the possible answer</span>
  <span id="freeText15928468772678535883" style="display:none">Reviewed in January, 2012<br /><br />One of the things I admire about Barnes is the pared down nature of his writing. Every word counts. <br />The division of this novella into two parts also counts. The reader could start with Part Two and the book wouldn't be any less clear. In fact, possible answers to most of the questions raised at the end of the book can be found on rereading Part One. More enlightenment comes while rereading Part Two. <br /><br />(The following paragraph may contain spoilers) <br />As to the possible answers to the questions raised in Part Two: the mathematical solution referred to on page 14, <i>he, (Robson) being about to cause an increase of one in the human population, had decided it was his ethical duty to keep the planet's numbers constant</i> may supply a reason for Adrian's later suicide in addition to his own stated belief that since we don't ask to be born, we ought at least to be allowed to choose when to die. <br /><br />Suicide, and Robson's suicide in particular, preoccupied Adrian from early in the book. His use of the details of Robson's death as an example of the unreliability of history sums up Barnes entire theme, i.e., the dependance of history on the imperfections of memory and the inadequacies of documentation, as well as the personal baggage of the historian. <br /><br />The reliability of Tony Webster's account, and of the entire story, are beset by these difficulties. Tony frequently acknowledges his imperfect memory, he possesses only a fragment of Adrian's diary, and Barnes ensures that we are well aware of Tony's personal baggage. <br /><br />So there can only be the sense of an ending, and great unrest in the mind of the reader because of the accumulation of responsibility.<br /><br />We are left with the unanswerable question: is Tony the equivalent of the Serbian gunman mentioned in the early pages? Is Tony the trigger for all the tragedy?</span>
  <a data-text-id="15928468772678535883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10260272583814538800">What a wonderful wonderful novel. No, not a novel, or a novella; it was a poem, with rhythm, repetition, and cadence, looping back on itself. Yes, it can only be called a poem - a poem about time, about forgotten time, long gone cold. <br/><br/>Having laid off from new Booker winners after a traumatic experience with Adiga, I started on this book with a lot of trepidation. But I was drawn in from the first paragraph and the amazing childhood anecdotes seemed to be promising a night of unbroken reading! I </span>
  <span id="freeText10260272583814538800" style="display:none">What a wonderful wonderful novel. No, not a novel, or a novella; it was a poem, with rhythm, repetition, and cadence, looping back on itself. Yes, it can only be called a poem - a poem about time, about forgotten time, long gone cold. <br /><br />Having laid off from new Booker winners after a traumatic experience with Adiga, I started on this book with a lot of trepidation. But I was drawn in from the first paragraph and the amazing childhood anecdotes seemed to be promising a night of unbroken reading! I went through it loving every line and anguishing over how I could never write like this - A patchy memory of life laid out in scattered pieces, excruciating in what is left unsaid and what is perhaps falsely remembered.<br /><br /><br /><br />I finished the book in a day and promised this - 'Full review to follow.' I am thinking about this again today but maybe I'll fulfill this promise tomorrow? The book touched me on many personal levels and I want to do justice to it, maybe even read it once again before attempting an extensive review.</span>

<div id="review_2077004227" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2077004227" />
    <a title="Iris P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p"><img alt="Iris P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483406411p2/2641948.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2077004227?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iris P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iris P" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p">Iris P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=overdrive-library">overdrive-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=should-have-read-long-time-ago">should-have-read-long-time-ago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=critically-aclaimed">critically-aclaimed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2077004227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16833956503723826541"><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending' title='The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sense of an Ending</a><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending' title='The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1311704453s/10746542.jpg' alt='The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>It's probably safe to say that most of us have, at some point in our lives, done or said things we have come to regret. That phone call you made when you were extremely upset. That bitter email you shouldn't have written. That text message you were too prompt to send. <br/><br/>If you had the opportunity of visiting a younger version of yourself and review what that other you said, felt, discovered, did or didn't do, how do you think it would measure up against the older you? Wou</span>
  <span id="freeText16833956503723826541" style="display:none"><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending" title="The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sense of an Ending</a><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10746542.The_Sense_of_an_Ending" title="The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1311704453s/10746542.jpg" alt="The Sense of an Ending by Julian Barnes" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>It's probably safe to say that most of us have, at some point in our lives, done or said things we have come to regret. That phone call you made when you were extremely upset. That bitter email you shouldn't have written. That text message you were too prompt to send. <br><br>If you had the opportunity of visiting a younger version of yourself and review what that other you said, felt, discovered, did or didn't do, how do you think it would measure up against the older you? Would you be ashamed, impressed, bewildered or perhaps just merely entertained by his or her behavior? <br><br>Tony Webster, the protagonist of <i>The Sense of an Ending</i>, finds himself in his middle age, confronted with some things he did as a young man and the unexpected but horrific consequences that came as a result. <br><br>I love the themes Barnes explores in this compact but very intense novel. Is it at all possible to reconcile our version of past events with reality? Or are we basically storytellers, conveniently editing those parts of our past that we wish had never happened? Can we trust our own memories or do we need others to validate the idea of who we think we are/were? Is remorse a worthy emotion or, because of its sense of inevitably, almost always a fruitless exercise?<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1462.Julian_Barnes" title="Julian Barnes" rel="nofollow noopener">Julian Barnes</a> is quickly becoming one of my favorite authors. His introspective style of writing seems to jolt my brain in the best possible way. <i>The Sense Of An Ending</i> is an intriguing story that makes you consider how perception can significantly affect our sense of reality and the way we view ourselves and others. <br><br>A fantastic read!<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="16833956503723826541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1792899353?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307947727" href="/book/show/13536315-the-sense-of-an-ending">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=english">english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=booker-prize-winner">booker-prize-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1792899353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18289144475977436062">I feel like I've got an angel and devil on either shoulder, when it comes to this book. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1511233282i/24494337.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>The being on one shoulder has an unnaturally soprano voice, eyes to the heavens, saying things like: "Oh merciful holiness, what IS this wonderful prose, this succinct precision, o lord, the slow burn of mystery juxtaposed with the deep meditation on memory, our affect on the course of history, and our inability to fix things in the past, god bless and exalt this clever Julian Barnes for a story that I relish</span>
  <span id="freeText18289144475977436062" style="display:none">I feel like I've got an angel and devil on either shoulder, when it comes to this book. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1511233282i/24494337.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The being on one shoulder has an unnaturally soprano voice, eyes to the heavens, saying things like: "Oh merciful holiness, what IS this wonderful prose, this succinct precision, o lord, the slow burn of mystery juxtaposed with the deep meditation on memory, our affect on the course of history, and our inability to fix things in the past, god bless and exalt this clever Julian Barnes for a story that I relished and delighted in! Ahhhh-mennnnnnn."<br><br>Then on the other shoulder, is a hulking, scowling, red-scorched jerk, uttering guttural complaints whilst twirling his pitchfork: "Are we supposed to believe ANY of the crap that we learned in the last two pages? That Tony was actually <i>responsible</i> for any of this? Are the other players in this story actually victims of his written word?? How is Tony supposed to understand anything when Veronica is so annoyingly obtuse, wordless and furious? And why would The Mother leave him anything in her GD will in the first place? Could this story be any more contrived? Hell and damnation!!"<br><br>I'm happy to report that the good guys won this particular battle, and I'm evening out my rating at a slightly miffed 4, because, despite all the hellish rumblings, I loved reading this book. Bravo to Julian Barnes, for elevating a problematic story with heavenly writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18289144475977436062" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
